reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "gpt2"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 32
batch_size = 1024

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

Let me check... Early GPT2 in transformers used `Conv1D` which is actually a linear layer with weights transposed compared to `nn.Linear`. The weight shape for `Conv1D` is `[in_features, out_features]`, and the computation is `x @ weight + bias`. Wait, actually, in the `transformers` library, GPT2 uses `Conv1D`: ```python class Conv1D(nn.Module): def __init__(self, nf, nx): super().__init__() self.nf = nf self.weight = Parameter(torch.empty(nx, nf)) self.bias = Parameter(torch.zeros(nf)) def forward(self, x): size_out = x.size()[:-1] + (self.nf,) x = torch.addmm(self.bias, x.view(-1, x.size(-1)), self.weight) x = x.view(size_out) return x ``` So the weight matrix is `[nx, nf]` and the matmul is `x @ W + bias`. This is equivalent to `nn.Linear` but with the weight transposed (or rather, stored in the transposed shape). In cuBLAS, we do C = A * B + C. If `x` is [M, K] and `W` is [K, N], then we compute C = x @ W. In cuBLAS with column-major, this is C += A^T @ B (with appropriate transposes). Actually, cuBLAS uses column-major by default, and PyTorch tensors are row-major. ATen's `addmm` handles this. For raw cuBLAS, I need to be careful about transposes. For row-major matrices [M, K] and [K, N], to compute C = A @ B where C is [M, N]: In cuBLAS column-major terms: - A_col = A^T which is [K, M] - B_col = B^T which is [N, K] - C_col = C^T which is [N, M] - We want C_col = B_col^T @ A_col^T = B @ A Using cuBLAS: - `cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, N, A, K, beta, C, N)` Wait, for row-major `C = A @ B`: - A is [M, K] row-major - B is [K, N] row-major - C is [M, N] row-major - In column-major (viewing the same data): - A becomes [K, M] with lda = K - B becomes [N, K] with ldb = N - C becomes [N, M] with ldc = N - Column-major formula: C = op(B) @ op(A) - So C_col = B_col^T @ A_col^T gives [K, N]^T @ [M, K]^T = [N, K] @ [K, M] = [N, M] ✓ That means for row-major, we call: `cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, 1.0, B, N, A, K, beta, C, N)` Wait, I think I'm messing this up. Let me be more careful. If we view a row-major [M, K] matrix as column-major, it looks like a [K, M] matrix. The leading dimension in column-major is the number of rows in the column-major interpretation. For row-major A[M][K], the elements are stored as A[i][j] = data[i * K + j]. In column-major, this same memory layout corresponds to a K × M matrix where element (j, i) is at offset i * K + j. So in column-major terms, this is the transpose.lda = K (the "height" in the original row-major terms, which is the dimension we stride by to get to the next column in column-major interpretation). Actually, the standard trick is: to multiply two row-major matrices A[M,K] and B[K,N] to get C[M,N] using column-major cuBLAS: - We compute C^T = B^T @ A^T in column-major - Which means we compute C = A @ B by calling: `cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, N, A, K, beta, C, N)` Wait no. Let's trace it: Column-major op(A) where A is actually our row-major B^T... this is getting confusing. Let me use the standard formula: For row-major `C = alpha * A * B + beta * C`: A is M×K, B is K×N, C is M×N. Call: `cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, N, A, K, beta, C, N)` Let's verify dimensions: - `CUBLAS_OP_N` means no transpose - First matrix (in col-major) is B with dimensions N×K - Second matrix (in col-major) is A with dimensions K×M - Result is C with dimensions N×M In col-major: C_col = B_col * A_col B_col is N×K, A_col is K×M, so C_col is N×M. But our row-major C is M×N. And col-major N×M corresponds to row-major M×N. Good! So: - B_col (N×K) viewed as row-major is K×N. That's our B matrix! ✓ - A_col (K×M) viewed as row-major is M×K. That's our A matrix! ✓ - C_col (N×M) viewed as row-major is M×N. That's our C matrix! ✓ So the call is correct: `cublasSgemm(..., CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, ..., B, N, A, K, ..., C, N)` Great, now I have the cuBLAS call figured out. For strided batched GEMM: `cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, N, M, K, alpha, B, ldb, strideB, A, lda, strideA, beta, C, ldc, strideC, batchCount)` Where: - B is our [batch, K, N] in row-major → col-major is [batch, N, K] - A is our [batch, M, K] in row-major → col-major is [batch, K, M] - C is our [batch, M, N] in row-major → col-major is [batch, N, M] - For row-major: ldb = N, lda = K, ldc = N - strideB = K*N, strideA = M*K, strideC = M*N Wait, actually for attention: Q is [B, nh, T, hs] = [1024, 12, 32, 64] K is [1024, 12, 32, 64] Q @ K^T is [1024, 12, 32, 32] To use strided batched: A (Q) dimensions for each batch: M=T=32, K=hs=64 B (K^T) dimensions: K=hs=64, N=T=32 Wait, K is [T, hs] row-major, K^T is [hs, T] row-major. Actually, to compute Q @ K^T: Q is [B*nh, T, hs] K^T is [B*nh, hs, T] So: M = T = 32 K = hs = 64 N = T = 32 batch = B*nh = 1024*12 = 12288 A = Q, shape [12288, 32, 64], lda = 64 B = K, shape [12288, 64, 32], ldb = 32 C = scores, shape [12288, 32, 32], ldc = 32 Using the formula: `cublasSgemmStridedBatched(..., N, M, K, ..., B, ldb, strideB, A, lda, strideA, ..., C, ldc, strideC, batch)` = `cublasSgemmStridedBatched(..., 32, 32, 64, ..., K, 32, 64*32, Q, 64, 32*64, ..., scores, 32, 32*32, 12288)` For attn @ V: attn_weights is [12288, 32, 32] V is [12288, 32, 64] output is [12288, 32, 64] M = 32, K = 32, N = 64 A = attn, lda = 32 B = V, ldb = 64 C = output, ldc = 64 Call: `cublasSgemmStridedBatched(..., 64, 32, 32, ..., V, 64, 32*64, attn, 32, 32*32, ..., out, 64, 32*64, 12288)` Good. For the feed-forward: c_fc: [B*T, n_embd] @ [n_embd, 4*n_embd] = [32768, 768] @ [768, 3072] → [32768, 3072] M = 32768, K = 768, N = 3072 Use cublasSgemm c_proj: [B*T, 4*n_embd] @ [4*n_embd, n_embd] = [32768, 3072] @ [3072, 768] → [32768, 768] LM head: [32768, 768] @ [768, 50257] → [32768, 50257] M = 32768, K = 768, N = 50257 This last one is huge! Now, for the attention, I could also fuse the scaling, masking, and softmax into one kernel. That would avoid writing `attn_weights` to global memory and reading it back. For T=32, it might not matter much, but every little bit helps. Similarly, I can fuse bias addition + residual add for some operations. Let me think about what kernels I need: 1. `embed_kernel`: lookup wte and wpe, add them 2. `layer_norm_kernel`: compute mean and variance, normalize, scale and shift 3. `add_bias_kernel`: add bias to matmul output 4. `split_qkv_kernel`: split [B*T, 3*n_embd] into Q, K, V 5. `attn_softmax_kernel`: scale, add mask, softmax 6. `gelu_kernel`: apply GELU 7. `add_residual_kernel`: add two tensors 8. Some combination kernels like `bias_add_residual` Actually, `split_qkv_kernel` can be done with a strided copy or we can just set up the cuBLAS QKV projection so that the output is already split in memory. But with cublasSgemm producing a [M, 3*N] matrix, splitting just involves pointer arithmetic if we compute separate strides. Actually, Q, K, V are all nh*hs = n_embd. So the c_attn output is 3*n_embd. For [32768, 3*768], Q is at offset 0, K is at offset 768, V is at offset 1536. We can just pass these pointers directly to the attention kernels without needing a copy! Similarly, the attention output from c_proj is [32768, 768], which we can add to the residual. Let me also think about memory layout. For the transformer blocks, each layer needs: - input (residual) - after ln1 - qkv - q, k, v (views into qkv) - attn_scores - attn_output - after c_proj - after add residual - after ln2 - after c_fc - after gelu - after c_proj - output (add residual) That's a lot of temporaries. Instead of allocating fresh memory for each, I can use a workspace buffer with enough space and reuse it. For B*T = 32768 and D = 768: - One activation of size [32768, 768] = 100,663,296 floats = 96 MB - For 12 layers, if we allocate one buffer for the main residual and swap two buffers, plus workspace for qkv and mlp and attention, we might need ~500 MB total. That's fine on H100 with 80GB. Now, for the attention softmax and masking, I need a custom kernel. For each [batch, T, T] attention scores: ```c++ for (int b = 0; b < batch; b++) { for (int i = 0; i < T; i++) { // For position i, masked positions are j > i float max_val = -INFINITY; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { max_val = max(max_val, scores[b][i][j]); } float sum = 0; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { scores[b][i][j] = exp(scores[b][i][j] - max_val); sum += scores[b][i][j]; } for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { scores[b][i][j] /= sum; } for (int j = i+1; j < T; j++) { scores[b][i][j] = 0; // or just leave it since we'll mask properly } } } ``` Actually, for numerical stability and to match HF exactly: HF does: `attn_weights += attention_mask` where attention_mask has 0 for valid and -10000 for masked. Then `attn_weights = softmax(attn_weights, dim=-1)` So the masked values are exp(-10000) which is effectively 0. But in practice, the max_val computation would still include them if not careful. In my kernel, I should: 1. Compute max over j=0..i (unmasked positions only) 2. Compute exp(x - max_val) for j=0..i 3. Set masked positions to 0 4. Divide by sum Here's a custom kernel for this: ```c++ __global__ void attn_softmax_kernel(float* scores, int batch, int T, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; if (b >= batch || i >= T) return; float* row = scores + b * T * T + i * T; // Find max over unmasked positions float max_val = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, row[j] * scale); } // Compute softmax float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { row[j] = expf(row[j] * scale - max_val); sum += row[j]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { row[j] *= inv_sum; } for (int j = i + 1; j < T; j++) { row[j] = 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, for the scale: in GPT2, the scale is `1.0f / sqrtf(hs)`. For hs=64, that's 0.125. Now, there's a subtlety: HF's softmax includes the masked values in the max computation if the mask value is -10000, because it adds the mask before softmax. But since -10000 is much smaller than typical values, it effectively doesn't affect the max. My approach of only looking at unmasked positions should produce the same result. Actually, there might be tiny differences if the actual attention scores are also very negative. But for GPT2 with typical weights, this should be fine. For GELU_new, I need to match HF exactly: ```python 0.5 * x * (1.0 + torch.tanh(math.sqrt(2.0 / math.pi) * (x + 0.044715 * torch.pow(x, 3.0)))) ``` ```c++ __device__ inline float gelu_new(float x) { float x3 = x * x * x; return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x3))); } ``` Note: `sqrt(2/pi) ≈ 0.7978845608028654` For layer norm: ```python mean = hidden_states.mean(-1, keepdim=True) variance = hidden_states.pow(2).mean(-1, keepdim=True) - mean**2 # or use .var with unbiased=False hidden_states = (hidden_states - mean) / torch.sqrt(variance + self.eps) hidden_states = self.weight * hidden_states + self.bias ``` HF uses `hidden_states = (hidden_states - mean) * torch.rsqrt(variance + self.eps) * self.weight + self.bias` ```c++ __global__ void layer_norm_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int N, int D, float eps) { int idx = blockIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; const float* x = in + idx * D; float* y = out + idx * D; float mean = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= D; float var = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) { float diff = x[i] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` For embedding: ```c++ __global__ void embed_kernel(float* out, const int* input_ids, const float* wte, const float* wpe, int B, int T, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int t = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B || t >= T) return; int token_id = input_ids[b * T + t]; const float* token_embed = wte + token_id * D; const float* pos_embed = wpe + t * D; float* out_ptr = out + (b * T + t) * D; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { out_ptr[i] = token_embed[i] + pos_embed[i]; } } ``` For bias add: ```c++ __global__ void add_bias_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, int N, int D) { int idx = blockIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; float* o = out + idx * D; const float* x = in + idx * D; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` For residual add: ```c++ __global__ void add_kernel(float* out, const float* a, const float* b, int N) { int idx = (blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x); if (idx >= N) return; out[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx]; } ``` For GELU: ```c++ __global__ void gelu_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int N) { int idx = (blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x); if (idx >= N) return; out[idx] = gelu_new(in[idx]); } ``` For attention output projection, after the strided batched GEMM, the output is in the format [B*nh, T, hs] = [12288, 32, 64]. But we need [B, T, D] = [1024, 32, 768] for the residual add. The layout of the cuBLAS output for attn @ V: Input attn: [batch=B*nh, T, T] Input V: [batch=B*nh, T, hs] Output: [batch=B*nh, T, hs] This is contiguous in memory as [B, nh, T, hs] if we view it that way. Actually, wait. The strided batched output is contiguous per batch, and batches are contiguous. So the memory layout is naturally [B*nh, T, hs]. But we need to transpose/reshape from [B, nh, T, hs] back to [B, T, nh, hs] = [B, T, D]. This requires a permutation: swap axes 1 and 2. I can write a `transpose_0213_kernel`: ```c++ __global__ void transpose_0213_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int B, int T, int nh, int hs) { int b = blockIdx.x; int t = blockIdx.y; if (b >= B || t >= T) return; const float* in_ptr = in + b * nh * T * hs + t * hs; float* out_ptr = out + (b * T + t) * D; for (int h = 0; h < nh; h++) { for (int d = threadIdx.x; d < hs; d += blockDim.x) { out_ptr[h * hs + d] = in_ptr[h * T * hs + d]; } } } ``` Wait, this is getting complicated. Actually, looking at the cuBLAS output layout: For `cublasSgemmStridedBatched`, C is [batch, M, N] row-major. Here M=T, N=hs. So C[b][t][d] is at offset b * T * hs + t * hs + d. But we want [B, T, D] where D = nh * hs, and the ordering is [B, T, nh, hs]. So at position (b, t, h, d): - In cuBLAS output: offset = (b * nh + h) * T * hs + t * hs + d - In target: offset = b * T * D + t * D + h * hs + d Yes, this is a transpose_0213 operation. ```c++ __global__ void transpose_0213(float* out, const float* in, int B, int T, int nh, int hs) { int total = B * T * nh * hs; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % hs; int h = (idx / hs) % nh; int t = (idx / (hs * nh)) % T; int b = idx / (hs * nh * T); int in_idx = ((b * nh + h) * T + t) * hs + d; int out_idx = ((b * T + t) * nh + h) * hs + d; out[out_idx] = in[in_idx]; } ``` Similarly, for the QKV projection, the output is [B*T, 3*D]. We need Q, K, V as [B, nh, T, hs]. Actually, the QKV projection output from cuBLAS is [32768, 2304] in row-major. Q is at column offset 0, length 768. K is at column offset 768, length 768. V is at column offset 1536, length 768. For Q: we need to reshape [B*T, D] to [B, T, D] and then transpose to [B, nh, T, hs]. For Q @ K^T, we need Q in [B, nh, T, hs]. So I need a kernel that takes [B*T, D] and produces [B, nh, T, hs]: ```c++ __global__ void reshape_split_transpose(float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* qkv, int B, int T, int D, int nh, int hs) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * T * D; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % D; int t = (idx / D) % T; int b = idx / (D * T); int h = d / hs; int hd = d % hs; int out_idx = ((b * nh + h) * T + t) * hs + hd; if (d < D) { q[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else if (d < 2*D) { k[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else { v[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } } ``` Wait, the input `qkv` is [B*T, 3*D] but in memory it's just [32768, 2304]. offset = (b*T + t) * 3*D + d where d is 0..3*D-1. For Q: d from 0 to D-1. For K: d from D to 2*D-1. For V: d from 2*D to 3*D-1. So: ```c++ int bt = idx / (3*D); int d = idx % (3*D); ``` Let me rewrite: ```c++ __global__ void split_qkv_kernel(float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* qkv, int B, int T, int D, int nh, int hs) { int total = B * T * 3 * D; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int d_total = idx % (3 * D); int bt = idx / (3 * D); int t = bt % T; int b = bt / T; int d = d_total % D; int h = d / hs; int hd = d % hs; int out_idx = ((b * nh + h) * T + t) * hs + hd; if (d_total < D) { q[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else if (d_total < 2 * D) { k[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else { v[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } } ``` This gives Q, K, V all in [B, nh, T, hs] layout, which is exactly what the batched GEMM needs. Similarly, for the MLP, after the first matmul we have [B*T, 4*D], then GELU, then the second matmul gives [B*T, D]. No reshaping needed. Actually wait, is that right? c_fc: [B*T, D] @ [D, 4*D] → [B*T, 4*D] c_proj: [B*T, 4*D] @ [4*D, D] → [B*T, D] Yes, these are simple. For the LM head: [B*T, D] @ [D, vocab_size] → [B*T, vocab_size] The weight is the transpose of wte. In HF GPT2, `lm_head.weight = wte.weight` (tied embeddings). And `lm_head` is typically Conv1D, so the weight is [D, vocab_size]. Actually, `lm_head` is a `nn.Linear` in newer transformers, or a `Conv1D` in older ones. For GPT2, it's a linear layer from hidden_size to vocab_size. The weight is typically [vocab_size, D] for nn.Linear, but since Conv1D stores [in_features, out_features], and GPT2 uses Conv1D for lm_head in older versions... Actually, let me check. In modern transformers, GPT2LMHeadModel uses: ```python self.lm_head = nn.Linear(config.n_embd, config.vocab_size, bias=False) # and ties weights with wte ``` Actually no, looking at the source: ```python class GPT2LMHeadModel(GPT2PreTrainedModel): def __init__(self, config): super().__init__(config) self.transformer = GPT2Model(config) self.lm_head = nn.Linear(config.n_embd, config.vocab_size, bias=False) # Initialize weights and apply final processing self.post_init() ``` Wait, I'm not sure. Let me look at the reference more carefully. The reference is: ```python self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config) def forward(self, x): return self.model(x).logits ``` For GPT2, `AutoModelForCausalLM` gives `GPT2LMHeadModel`. The logits are computed as `self.lm_head(self.transformer(x).last_hidden_state)`. If I extract the weights, I need to be careful about whether `lm_head` is `nn.Linear` or `Conv1D`. For `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)`: - weight shape is [out_features, in_features] - computation is `x @ weight.t() + bias` For `Conv1D(nf, nx)`: - weight shape is [nx, nf] = [in_features, out_features] - computation is `x @ weight + bias` In modern transformers (4.x), GPT2 uses `Conv1D` for the attention and MLP weights, but `lm_head` changed from `Conv1D` to `nn.Linear` at some point. Actually, I should extract the weights dynamically and figure out their shapes at runtime. This way I handle either case. In Python, I can do: ```python # For c_attn self.c_attn_weight = model.transformer.h[i].attn.c_attn.weight # [D, 3*D] for Conv1D or [3*D, D] for Linear self.c_attn_bias = model.transformer.h[i].attn.c_attn.bias # [3*D] ``` And then in the CUDA code, for cuBLAS: If weight is [D, 3*D] (Conv1D), then `x @ weight` is [M, D] @ [D, 3*D] which is standard. If weight is [3*D, D] (Linear), then `x @ weight.t()` is [M, D] @ [D, 3*D]. So I need to transpose if it's a Linear layer. But for simplicity, I can transpose the weights in Python before passing to CUDA. ```python def extract_conv1d_or_linear(module): if isinstance(module, torch.nn.Linear): return module.weight.t().contiguous(), module.bias else: # Conv1D return module.weight.contiguous(), module.bias ``` Actually, `Conv1D` stores weight as [nx, nf] and does `x @ weight + bias`. This is the same as a Linear layer with weight transposed. So if I get the weight from Conv1D, it's already in the right shape for `x @ weight`. For `nn.Linear`, weight is [out_features, in_features], so I need to transpose it to get [in_features, out_features]. Let me write a helper: ```python def get_weight(w): if hasattr(w, 'weight'): weight = w.weight if weight.shape[0] == weight.shape[1]: # Square, ambiguous pass # For Linear: [out, in] # For Conv1D: [in, out] # We want [in, out] if isinstance(w, torch.nn.Linear): return weight.t().contiguous() else: return weight.contiguous() ``` Actually, a robust way is: ```python # Check the module type if 'Conv1D' in type(module).__name__: weight = module.weight # [in, out] bias = module.bias # [out] return weight, bias elif isinstance(module, torch.nn.Linear): weight = module.weight.t() # [in, out] bias = module.bias return weight, bias ``` For GPT2, c_attn is Conv1D. The lm_head might be Linear or Conv1D depending on the transformers version. I'll handle both. Also, there's the `lm_head` weight tying. In HF, `lm_head.weight` is often the same tensor as `wte.weight`. For our purposes, we can just extract it. One more thing: position embeddings. GPT2 uses learned position embeddings up to `n_positions=1024`. The input positions are just `torch.arange(T)` expanded to [B, T]. For seq_len=32, this is always 0..31. Okay, now let me think about the overall structure of the C++ code. Actually, another important consideration: how do I pass all the weights to the CUDA function? There are many weights: - wte, wpe - For 12 layers: ln_1.g, ln_1.b, c_attn.w, c_attn.b, c_proj.w, c_proj.b, ln_2.g, ln_2.b, c_fc.w, c_fc.b, c_proj.w, c_proj.b - ln_f.g, ln_f.b - lm_head.w That's 2 + 12*12 + 2 = 148 weight tensors. I could pass them all as separate arguments to the CUDA function, but that's unwieldy. Instead, I can register them as buffers in `ModelNew` and in `forward`, pass them one by one to a C++ function, OR I can concatenate them into a few big tensors. Actually, the simplest approach for `load_inline` is to pass them all as arguments to the C++ function. But pybind11 has an argument limit. A better approach: store them in `ModelNew`, and in the C++ forward function, accept `std::vector<at::Tensor>` or use multiple arguments. Wait, `load_inline` with `functions=['gpt2_forward']` will auto-generate a pybind11 wrapper for `gpt2_forward`. If `gpt2_forward` takes `(at::Tensor input_ids, at::Tensor wte, ... lots of tensors ...)`, that's fine as long as it's < 64 arguments or whatever pybind11 limit is. For 148 tensors, we'd exceed typical limits. So I should use `std::vector<at::Tensor>`: ```cpp at::Tensor gpt2_forward(at::Tensor input_ids, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights) { // weights[0] = wte // weights[1] = wpe // etc. } ``` In Python: ```python logits = module.gpt2_forward(x, self.all_weights) ``` Yes, this is much cleaner. Now for workspace management. In the C++ function, I can allocate all temporary tensors using `at::empty` or `at::zeros`. ATen will handle memory management via RAII. This is actually great because I don't need to manually manage CUDA memory. Let me draft the workspace allocation in C++: ```cpp at::Tensor gpt2_forward(at::Tensor input_ids, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights) { int B = 1024; // Actually, get from input_ids.size(0) int T = 32; // input_ids.size(1) int D = 768; int nh = 12; int hs = 64; int n_layer = 12; int vocab_size = 50257; // Get CUDA stream cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); cublasHandle_t handle = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle(); cublasSetStream(handle, stream); // Embedding auto hidden = at::empty({B, T, D}, input_ids.options().dtype(at::kFloat)); embed_kernel<<<grid, block, 0, stream>>>(...); // Main loop ping-ponging between two buffers auto buf0 = hidden; auto buf1 = at::empty({B, T, D}, at::kFloat); auto* current = buf0.data_ptr<float>(); auto* next = buf1.data_ptr<float>(); auto qkv = at::empty({B*T, 3*D}, at::kFloat); auto attn_scores = at::empty({B*nh, T, T}, at::kFloat); auto attn_out = at::empty({B*nh, T, hs}, at::kFloat); auto mlp_mid = at::empty({B*T, 4*D}, at::kFloat); for (int layer = 0; layer < n_layer; layer++) { // Extract weights for this layer // ... // LayerNorm 1 layer_norm_kernel<<<grid_ln, block_ln, 0, stream>>>(next, current, ln1_g, ln1_b, B*T, D); std::swap(current, next); // QKV projection // cuBLAS: [B*T, D] @ [D, 3*D] -> qkv float alpha = 1.0f, beta = 0.0f; cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, 3*D, B*T, D, &alpha, c_attn_w, 3*D, current, D, &beta, qkv.data_ptr<float>(), 3*D); add_bias_kernel<<<grid_bias, block_bias, 0, stream>>>(qkv.data_ptr<float>(), qkv.data_ptr<float>(), c_attn_b, B*T, 3*D); // Split and transpose Q, K, V // ... // Attention scores cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_T, CUBLAS_OP_N, T, T, hs, &alpha, k_ptr, hs, T*hs, q_ptr, hs, T*hs, &beta, attn_scores.data_ptr<float>(), T, T*T, B*nh); // Softmax attn_softmax_kernel<<<grid_softmax, 1, 0, stream>>>(attn_scores.data_ptr<float>(), B*nh, T, 1.0f/sqrtf(hs)); // Attention @ V cublasSgemmStridedBatched(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, hs, T, T, &alpha, v_ptr, hs, T*hs, attn_scores.data_ptr<float>(), T, T*T, &beta, attn_out.data_ptr<float>(), hs, T*hs, B*nh); // Transpose back and project transpose_0213<<<grid_trans, block_trans, 0, stream>>>(...); cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, D, B*T, D, &alpha, c_proj_w, D, attn_buf_ptr, D, &beta, next, D); add_bias_kernel<<<...>>>(next, next, c_proj_b, B*T, D); add_kernel<<<...>>>(next, next, current_residual, B*T*D); std::swap(current, next); // LayerNorm 2 // ... // MLP cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, 4*D, B*T, D, &alpha, c_fc_w, 4*D, current, D, &beta, mlp_mid.data_ptr<float>(), 4*D); add_bias_and_gelu_kernel<<<...>>>(mlp_mid.data_ptr<float>(), mlp_mid.data_ptr<float>(), c_fc_b, B*T*4*D); cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, D, B*T, 4*D, &alpha, c_proj_w, D, mlp_mid.data_ptr<float>(), 4*D, &beta, next, D); add_bias_and_residual_kernel<<<...>>>(next, next, c_proj_b, current_residual, B*T*D); std::swap(current, next); } // Final layer norm layer_norm_kernel<<<...>>>(next, current, ln_f_g, ln_f_b, B*T, D); // LM head auto logits = at::empty({B*T, vocab_size}, at::kFloat); cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, vocab_size, B*T, D, &alpha, lm_head_w, vocab_size, next, D, &beta, logits.data_ptr<float>(), vocab_size); return logits.view({B, T, vocab_size}); } ``` This is very doable! The cuBLAS calls are straightforward, and the custom kernels are simple. Actually, I need to handle the residuals carefully. After each layer's main path and MLP path, I need to add the residual. Let me trace through more carefully: For each layer: 1. Input x 2. ln1(x) -> y1 3. attn(y1) -> a 4. x + a -> r1 5. ln2(r1) -> y2 6. mlp(y2) -> m 7. r1 + m -> output So I need to keep the residual around. In my code: ```cpp // current = x // next = workspace // ln1(current) -> next layer_norm<<<...>>>(next, current, ...); swap(current, next); // current = ln1(x) // attn(current) -> qkv -> ... -> next (attn output) // next = attn(ln1(x)) // add residual: next = next + residual (which was the original x) // So I need to save `residual` = original x before ln1 // Let's restructure: auto residual = current; layer_norm<<<...>>>(next, current, ...); swap(current, next); // current = ln1(x) // ... attention on current, write to next ... // next = attn(current) + residual // swap(current, next) -> current = attn(ln1(x)) + x // Now residual = current residual = current; layer_norm<<<...>>>(next, current, ...); swap(current, next); // current = ln2(r1) // ... mlp on current, write to next with residual add ... // next = mlp(ln2(r1)) + r1 // swap -> current = output of layer ``` So I need `residual` to be a pointer that I update, or I can just use `current` and `next` plus a saved pointer. Actually, I can just do: ```cpp float* residual = current; // ln1 layer_norm(next, current, ...); swap(current, next); // attn -> next // next += residual add_residual(next, current_residual=residual); swap(current, next); residual = current; // ln2 layer_norm(next, current, ...); swap(current, next); // mlp -> next, with residual add // At the end of mlp, before swap: next += residual mlp_with_residual(next, current, ..., residual); swap(current, next); ``` Wait, I need to be more careful. After the first swap, `current = ln1(x)` and `next = original x`. After attention writes to `next`, `next = attn(ln1(x))`. Then `next += original_x`. But `original_x` is in `current` BEFORE we did the first swap? No, after the first swap, `next` holds `original_x`. Let me track it: - Initially: `current` = x, `next` = garbage - `layer_norm(next, current)` → `next` = ln1(x) - `swap`: `current` = ln1(x), `next` = x (the original, unchanged!) - `attn(next, current)` → Wait, I want attn output to go somewhere. - Better: have `qkv` and `attn_out` as separate buffers. The c_proj writes to `next`. - So `next` = c_proj(attn_out) - `add_bias(next, c_proj_b)` - `add(next, next, original_x)` where original_x is now in `next` after swap? No, after swap `next` = x. Wait, I need three buffers for ping-pong plus workspace: - `buf0`, `buf1` for main activations - `residual_ptr` points to the buffer that holds the input to the current sublayer Actually, let me just use three buffers: `act`, `tmp`, `residual`. ```cpp auto act = embed_output; // [B, T, D] auto tmp = at::empty_like(act); auto residual = at::empty_like(act); for each layer: // Save residual copy(residual, act); // or just residual = act.data_ptr(), because act won't be overwritten until we're done with it // LayerNorm 1: tmp = ln1(act) layer_norm(tmp.data_ptr(), act.data_ptr(), ...); // Attention: act = attn(tmp) cublas(qkv, tmp, c_attn_w); add_bias(qkv, c_attn_b); split_qkv(q, k, v, qkv); cublas(attn_scores, q, k); softmax(attn_scores); cublas(attn_out, attn_scores, v); transpose_0213(tmp2, attn_out); // tmp2 is [B*T, D] cublas(act, tmp2, c_proj_w); // act = c_proj(transposed_attn) add_bias(act, c_proj_b); add(act, act, residual); // act = attn + residual // Save residual copy(residual, act); // LayerNorm 2: tmp = ln2(act) layer_norm(tmp.data_ptr(), act.data_ptr(), ...); // MLP: act = mlp(tmp) cublas(mlp_mid, tmp, c_fc_w); add_bias_and_gelu(mlp_mid, c_fc_b); cublas(act, mlp_mid, c_proj_w); add_bias(act, c_proj_b); add(act, act, residual); // act = mlp + residual ``` This requires: - `act`: [B, T, D] current activation - `tmp`: [B, T, D] workspace for ln output - `tmp2`: [B, T, D] workspace for attention output before c_proj - `residual`: [B, T, D] saved residual - `qkv`: [B*T, 3*D] - `attn_scores`: [B*nh, T, T] - `attn_out`: [B*nh, T, hs] - `mlp_mid`: [B*T, 4*D] Wait, I can reuse `tmp` as `tmp2` since they're not needed at the same time. After `layer_norm(tmp, act)`, `tmp` holds ln1 output. Then attention computes into various buffers, and the final attention projection can go into `act` directly. Actually, after attention, I want `act = c_proj(attn_out) + residual`. The `residual` is the original `act` before any operations. `tmp` is used for `ln1(act)`. For the attention path: 1. `cublas(qkv, tmp, c_attn_w)` → qkv = c_attn(tmp) 2. `split` → q, k, v 3. `cublas(attn_scores, q, k)` 4. `softmax(attn_scores)` 5. `cublas(attn_out, attn_scores, v)` 6. `tmp = transpose_0213(attn_out)` or could directly use another workspace 7. `cublas(act, workspace_attn, c_proj_w)` → act = c_proj(attn_result) 8. `add_bias(act, c_proj_b)` 9. `add(act, act, residual)` → act += residual Here, `residual` should be the original `act` before step 1. `workspace_attn` is the transposed attention output [B*T, D]. So I need: - `act`: [B, T, D] - main activation, also holds residual path - `tmp`: [B, T, D] - workspace for layer norm and potentially transposed attn - `residual`: [B, T, D] - saved copy of act before sublayer - `qkv`: [B*T, 3*D] - `attn_scores`: [B*nh, T, T] - `attn_out`: [B*nh, T, hs] - `mlp_mid`: [B*T, 4*D] Can I reduce this? - `residual` could just be a pointer to the old `act` buffer if I use triple buffering. - For `tmp`, can I avoid it? With triple buffering (buf0, buf1, buf2): ```cpp auto& x = buf0; // current input auto& ln_out = buf1; // ln1(x) auto& attn_proj = buf2; // c_proj(attn(...ln_out...)) // ... compute attn_proj from ln_out ... // ... add x to attn_proj ... // Now: attn_proj = x + attn(ln1(x)) // Next layer needs this as input ``` So for each layer, I cycle through the three buffers: Layer 0: x=buf0, ln=buf1, out=buf2 Layer 1: x=buf2, ln=buf0, out=buf1 Layer 2: x=buf1, ln=buf2, out=buf0 This means I don't need a separate `residual` buffer, because `x` (bufX) is not overwritten until the output is computed in another buffer. The sequence: 1. `x` = buf0 2. `layer_norm(ln_out=buf1, x=buf0)` 3. `attn(out=buf2, ln=buf1)` and `add(out=buf2, out=buf2, x=buf0)` After step 3, `buf2` holds the first sublayer output. Now: 4. `layer_norm(ln_out=buf0, x=buf2)` 5. `mlp(out=buf1, ln=buf0)` and `add(out=buf1, out=buf1, x=buf2)` After step 5, `buf1` holds the layer output. Next layer: 6. `layer_norm(ln_out=buf2, x=buf1)` ... etc. This is elegant! I only need 3 main buffers of size [B, T, D]. But wait, for the MLP: - `cublas(mlp_mid, ln, c_fc_w)` → mlp_mid [B*T, 4*D] - `gelu(mlp_mid)` - `cublas(out, mlp_mid, c_proj_w)` → out [B*T, D] - `add_bias(out, c_proj_b)` - `add(out, out, x)` where x is the layer input (residual) For `ln_out` in step 4, I'm using `buf0` which currently contains the first sublayer's `ln1(x)` from step 2. But after step 3, `buf2` is the sublayer output, and `buf0` and `buf1` are free to be reused. Actually wait, after step 2, `buf1` = ln1(x). Then step 3 uses `buf1` as input to attention, and writes the final result to `buf2`. The `buf0` is still the original x. Good. Then step 4 writes ln2(buf2) into `buf0`. Now `buf0` is overwritten, but the original x was in `buf0` and we already used it in step 3 for the residual add. So that's fine. Step 5: MLP operates on `buf0` (which holds ln2 output), and writes to `buf1`. Then `buf1 += buf2` (residual). Now `buf1` is the final layer output. For the next layer, we use `buf1` as x. `buf2` and `buf0` are available as workspace. So the buffer cycling works! Workspace requirements: - 3 x [B, T, D] main buffers - 1 x [B*T, 3*D] for qkv - 1 x [B*nh, T, T] for attn_scores - 1 x [B*nh, T, hs] for attn_out - 1 x [B*T, 4*D] for mlp_mid Total workspace: 3 * 96 MB + 288 MB + 48 MB + 48 MB + 384 MB = 288 + 768 = 1056 MB. Wait, let me recalculate: - B=1024, T=32, D=768 - [B, T, D] = 1024*32*768 = 25,165,824 floats = 96 MB - 3 buffers = 288 MB - qkv = 1024*32*3*768 = 75,497,472 floats = 288 MB - attn_scores = 1024*12*32*32 = 12,582,912 floats = 48 MB - attn_out = 1024*12*32*64 = 25,165,824 floats = 96 MB - mlp_mid = 1024*32*3072 = 100,663,296 floats = 384 MB Total: 288 + 288 + 48 + 96 + 384 = 1104 MB. That's about 1.1 GB. Fine for H100. Actually, can I reduce the qkv buffer size? Q, K, V each need [B, nh, T, hs] = 96 MB total. But qkv projection outputs [B*T, 3*D] which is also 288 MB. And Q, K, V need another 288 MB. Wait, B*nh*T*hs = 1024*12*32*64 = 25,165,824 = 96 MB per head group? No: 1024*12*32*64 = 25,165,824 floats total for Q. K is another 25,165,824. V is another 25,165,824. Total for Q+K+V = 75,497,472 floats = 288 MB. The qkv projection output is [B*T, 3*D] = 32768 * 2304 = 75,497,472 floats = 288 MB. So if I do an in-place split, I could potentially reuse the qkv buffer for Q, K, V views. But for the batched GEMM, Q, K, V need to be in [B*nh, T, hs] layout. The split_transpose kernel can read from qkv and write to separate Q, K, V buffers. So we need both qkv AND Q+K+V. That's 288 + 288 = 576 MB just for attention internals. Can I avoid this? If I write the split kernel so that qkv is consumed and produces Q, K, V in place... not easily, because the layout changes. Actually, I can over-allocate qkv as [B*nh, T, 3*hs] and have Q, K, V be contiguous with a specific strided layout. Let me think... For the batched GEMM, we need Q and K to have stride = T*hs between batches. If I allocate qkv as [B, nh, T, 3*hs], then: Q is at hs offset 0 K is at hs offset hs V is at hs offset 2*hs The slice for Q at batch (b, h) is [T, hs] with stride between (b, h) slices = T*3*hs. But the batched GEMM needs stride = T*hs. If I use `cublasSgemmBatched` (not strided) with an array of pointers, then each batch can have arbitrary location. That might be more efficient here. With `cublasSgemmBatched`, I prepare an array of pointers: - A_array[i] = &Q[i * T * hs] but Q for batch i = (b * nh + h) starts at offset (b * nh + h) * T * 3*hs + 0 * hs Wait no, Q has its own contiguous buffer. Actually, it's cleaner to just allocate separate Q, K, V buffers. The memory overhead is acceptable. One more optimization: for the tiny T=32, maybe I can write a fused attention kernel that takes [B*nh, T, hs] Q, K, V and directly computes the output [B*nh, T, hs] without the intermediate score matrix? For small T, I could load K and V into shared memory and compute for each query. Actually, for T=32 and hs=64, K is [32, 64] and V is [32, 64]. Each is 8K floats = 32 KB. For batch=12288, we'd need one tile at a time. But writing a custom batched attention kernel for arbitrary batch on an H100... it's doable but complex. I'll stick with cuBLAS + custom softmax kernel for now. Let me now think about the actual CUDA C++ code I need to write. Actually, I realize I should also consider writing the C++ code to use ATen's tensor operations where beneficial, and custom kernels where fusion matters. For example, `at::addcmul` or `at::layer_norm` could handle some ops. But the overhead of calling many ATen ops from C++ is still there. Actually, if I'm writing custom CUDA kernels anyway, I might as well write them for all the elementwise ops. Wait, I have another idea. What if I use CUTLASS or something? No, let's stick with cuBLAS + custom kernels. Let me also consider if I can use float16/bfloat16 internally with fp32 accumulation for the matmuls to get Tensor Core speedup. On H100, bf16 Tensor Cores are extremely fast. If I convert weights to bf16 and do the matmuls in bf16 with fp32 accumulation, then cast back to fp32 for the elementwise ops, I could see a ~4x speedup on the compute-bound matmuls. But would this pass the fp32 tolerance of 1e-4? bf16 has ~1e-3 relative precision. With large matrices, the accumulation error might be close to the tolerance. For 768-dim matmuls, bf16 accumulation to fp32 is quite accurate because the inner loop accumulates in fp32. PyTorch does bf16 matmuls with fp32 accumulation as `torch.matmul(bf16_a, bf16_b, out=fp32_c)`. The error is usually well within 1e-4 for most purposes, but there can be edge cases. Actually, the problem says "Produce numerically equivalent outputs at fp32 precision." This means the output dtype should be fp32. It doesn't strictly say we can't use lower precision internally. But the tolerance check for fp32 output is 1e-4. If we use bf16 for the matmuls, would we pass? For the eval, they compute reference with full fp32 (possibly using TF32 on Ampere/Hopper). If we use bf16, there might be differences that exceed 1e-4. To be safe, I'll stick with fp32 for the matmuls. On H100, fp32 matmuls use TF32 Tensor Cores by default when allowed, which gives a good speedup while maintaining reasonable precision. The reference PyTorch code will also use TF32 by default (since `torch.backends.cuda.matmul.allow_tf32` defaults to True on Hopper). So we're on equal footing. Actually wait, I should double check the TF32 defaults. Since 1.7, `allow_tf32` defaults to `True`. The reference model running in fp32 on H100 will use TF32 for the matmuls. My custom cuBLAS calls will also use TF32 because the default cuBLAS math mode allows it. So we'll match. Now, the key speedup will come from: 1. Eliminating Python overhead from the 12-layer loop in HuggingFace 2. Fusing elementwise ops (bias add, GELU, residual add) 3. Reducing memory allocations (pre-allocated workspace) 4. Better stream synchronization This could easily be 2-3x faster, maybe more! Let me start writing the code. First, the Python part: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig # CUDA C++ source cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cublas_v2.h> #include <math.h> #define B 1024 #define T 32 #define D 768 #define NH 12 #define HS 64 #define NLAYERS 12 #define VOCAB_SIZE 50257 #define MLP_DIM 3072 #define EPS 1e-5f __device__ inline float gelu_new(float x) { float x3 = x * x * x; return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + tanhf(0.7978845608f * (x + 0.044715f * x3))); } __global__ void embed_kernel(float* out, const int64_t* input_ids, const float* wte, const float* wpe, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x; int t = blockIdx.y; int idx = b * T + t; int token_id = (int)input_ids[idx]; const float* tok_emb = wte + token_id * D; const float* pos_emb = wpe + t * D; float* dst = out + idx * D; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { dst[i] = tok_emb[i] + pos_emb[i]; } } __global__ void layer_norm_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const float* x = in + n * D_val; float* y = out + n * D_val; float mean = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D_val; i++) mean += x[i]; mean /= D_val; float var = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D_val; i++) { float d = x[i] - mean; var += d * d; } var /= D_val; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + EPS); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } __global__ void add_bias_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; float* o = out + n * D_val; const float* x = in + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i]; } } __global__ void add_kernel(float* out, const float* a, const float* b, int N) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; out[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx]; } __global__ void gelu_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int N) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; out[idx] = gelu_new(in[idx]); } __global__ void add_bias_gelu_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; float* o = out + n * D_val; const float* x = in + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = gelu_new(x[i] + bias[i]); } } __global__ void split_qkv_kernel(float* q, float* k, float* v, const float* qkv, int B_val, int T_val, int D_val, int nh, int hs) { int total = B_val * T_val * 3 * D_val; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int d_total = idx % (3 * D_val); int bt = idx / (3 * D_val); int t = bt % T_val; int b = bt / T_val; int d = d_total % D_val; int h = d / hs; int hd = d % hs; int out_idx = ((b * nh + h) * T_val + t) * hs + hd; if (d_total < D_val) { q[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else if (d_total < 2 * D_val) { k[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } else { v[out_idx] = qkv[idx]; } } __global__ void attn_softmax_kernel(float* scores, int batch, int T_val, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; if (b >= batch || i >= T_val) return; float* row = scores + b * T_val * T_val + i * T_val; float max_val = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, row[j] * scale); } float sum = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { row[j] = expf(row[j] * scale - max_val); sum += row[j]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum; for (int j = 0; j <= i; j++) { row[j] *= inv_sum; } for (int j = i + 1; j < T_val; j++) { row[j] = 0.0f; } } __global__ void transpose_0213_kernel(float* out, const float* in, int B_val, int T_val, int nh, int hs) { int total = B_val * T_val * nh * hs; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int d = idx % hs; int h = (idx / hs) % nh; int t = (idx / (hs * nh)) % T_val; int b = idx / (hs * nh * T_val); int in_idx = ((b * nh + h) * T_val + t) * hs + d; out[idx] = in[in_idx]; } at::Tensor gpt2_forward(at::Tensor input_ids, std::vector<at::Tensor> weights) { // weights layout: // 0: wte // 1: wpe // 2: ln_f_g // 3: ln_f_b // 4: lm_head_w // Then for each layer i (0..11): // base = 5 + i * 10 // ln1_g, ln1_b, c_attn_w, c_attn_b, c_proj_w, c_proj_b, // ln2_g, ln2_b, c_fc_w, c_fc_b, mlp_c_proj_w, mlp_c_proj_b // Actually let me use a simpler indexing from Python side. cudaStream_t stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); cublasHandle_t handle = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDABlasHandle(); cublasSetStream(handle, stream); // Buffers auto opts = input_ids.options().dtype(at::kFloat); auto buf0 = at::empty({B, T, D}, opts); auto buf1 = at::empty({B, T, D}, opts); auto buf2 = at::empty({B, T, D}, opts); auto qkv = at::empty({B * T, 3 * D}, opts); auto attn_scores = at::empty({B * NH, T, T}, opts); auto attn_out = at::empty({B * NH, T, HS}, opts); auto mlp_mid = at::empty({B * T, MLP_DIM}, opts); // Embed embed_kernel<<<dim3(B, T), 256, 0, stream>>>( buf0.data_ptr<float>(), input_ids.data_ptr<int64_t>(), weights[0].data_ptr<float>(), weights[1].data_ptr<float>(), D); float alpha = 1.0f; float beta = 0.0f; float* x = buf0.data_ptr<float>(); float* ln_out = buf1.data_ptr<float>(); float* attn_proj = buf2.data_ptr<float>(); for (int layer = 0; layer < NLAYERS; layer++) { int wbase = 5 + layer * 12; float* ln1_g = weights[wbase].data_ptr<float>(); float* ln1_b = weights[wbase + 1].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_attn_w = weights[wbase + 2].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_attn_b = weights[wbase + 3].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_proj_w = weights[wbase + 4].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_proj_b = weights[wbase + 5].data_ptr<float>(); float* ln2_g = weights[wbase + 6].data_ptr<float>(); float* ln2_b = weights[wbase + 7].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_fc_w = weights[wbase + 8].data_ptr<float>(); float* c_fc_b = weights[wbase + 9].data_ptr<float>(); float* mlp_c_proj_w = weights[wbase + 10].data_ptr<float>(); float* mlp_c_proj_b = weights[wbase + 11].data_ptr<float>(); // Self-attention layer_norm_kernel<<<B * T, 256, 0, stream>>>(ln_out, x, ln1_g, ln1_b, B * T, D); // QKV cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, 3 * D, B * T, D, &alpha, c_attn_w, 3 * D, ln_out, D, &beta, qkv.data_ptr<float>(), 3 * D); add_bias_kernel<<<B * T, 256, 0, stream>>>(qkv.data_ptr<float>(), qkv.data_ptr<float>(), c_attn_b, B * T, 3 * D); // Split QKV int total_qkv = B * T * 3 * D; split_qkv_kernel<<<(total_qkv + 255) / 256, 256, 0, stream>>>( ln_out, ln_out + B * T * D, ln_out + 2 * B * T * D, qkv.data_ptr<float>(), B, T, D, NH, HS); // Wait, ln_out is [B*T, D]. We can't store Q, K, V there because it would overwrite. // We need separate Q, K, V buffers. Let me reallocate workspace. // Actually I should have dedicated Q,K,V buffers. Let me fix this. } return at::empty({1}, opts); // placeholder } ''' ``` Wait, I made a mistake. `ln_out` is only `B*T*D` in size. I can't store Q, K, V there. I need separate buffers for Q, K, V. Similarly, can we fuse `add_bias` + `add_residual`? ```cpp __global__ void add_bias_and_residual_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, const float* residual, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; float* o = out + n * D_val; const float* x = in + n * D_val; const float* r = residual + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i] + r[i]; } } ``` For `c_proj` after attention: `out = matmul_result + bias` For the residual add: `out = out + residual` So we can fuse these: ```cpp __global__ void attn_out_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, const float* residual, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; const float* x = in + n * D_val; const float* r = residual + n * D_val; float* o = out + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i] + r[i]; } } ``` For MLP output: ```cpp __global__ void mlp_out_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, const float* residual, int N, int D_val) { // same as above } ``` Actually, I should write a generic fused kernel: ```cpp __global__ void add3_kernel(float* out, const float* a, const float* b, const float* c, int N) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; out[idx] = a[idx] + b[idx] + c[idx]; } ``` But `a`, `b`, `c` need to be broadcasted over the batch dimension. For bias, it's the same for all batch elements. So I can't just add element-wise over the flattened array unless the bias is already broadcasted. Wait, for bias: it's [D], and the activations are [N, D]. So I need: ```cpp out[n*D + d] = in[n*D + d] + bias[d] + residual[n*D + d]; ``` This is different from a flat elementwise add where bias would be [N*D]. So the `add3_kernel` with broadcasting is: ```cpp __global__ void add_bias_residual_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, const float* residual, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; float* o = out + n * D_val; const float* x = in + n * D_val; const float* r = residual + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i] + r[i]; } } ``` Good. Let me check what types these are in HF GPT2. In `transformers` GPT2: - `wte`: `nn.Embedding` → weight is [vocab_size, n_embd] - `wpe`: `nn.Embedding` → weight is [n_positions, n_embd] - `ln_1`, `ln_2`, `ln_f`: `nn.LayerNorm` → weight and bias are [n_embd] - `attn.c_attn`: `Conv1D` → weight is [n_embd, 3*n_embd], bias is [3*n_embd] - `attn.c_proj`: `Conv1D` → weight is [n_embd, n_embd], bias is [n_embd] - `mlp.c_fc`: `Conv1D` → weight is [n_embd, 4*n_embd], bias is [4*n_embd] - `mlp.c_proj`: `Conv1D` → weight is [4*n_embd, n_embd], bias is [n_embd] - `lm_head`: `nn.Linear` in newer versions, weight is [vocab_size, n_embd] For the CUDA code, all matmuls are `x @ W` where x is [M, K] and W is [K, N]. So I need weights in [K, N] format. For Conv1D, weight is already [K, N] = [n_embd, out_features]. For nn.Linear, weight is [N, K], so I need to transpose. For nn.Embedding, the weight is [num_embeddings, embedding_dim]. For lookup, I'll just use the raw weight. For lm_head: If it's nn.Linear, weight is [vocab_size, n_embd]. For `x @ W^T`, we'd need to pass `W.t()` to CUDA where the matmul is `x @ W_t` with W_t = [n_embd, vocab_size]. So `weights[4] = lm_head.weight.t().contiguous()`. If it's Conv1D, weight is [n_embd, vocab_size], which is already correct. In the Python code, I'll handle both cases generically. Actually, the `load_inline` compilation might take a while because there's a lot of CUDA code. I should make sure the code compiles. Also, I should set `with_cuda=True` or similar, and maybe add flags. For pybind11, the vector of tensors argument might need `#include <vector>`. I'll include that. Also, I need to make sure all tensors are on the correct device before passing to the CUDA function. Let me also consider whether I should use a struct to pass the layer weights. But 149 tensors as a vector is fine. One more thing: I should ensure my kernels use enough threads per block to be efficient. For `layer_norm_kernel` with D=768, 256 threads is good (768/256 = 3 iterations per thread, or 768/128 = 6, etc.). For the `embed_kernel`, `blockDim.x = 256` means each block handles one (b, t) position, with threads collaborating to copy 768 floats. For `split_qkv_kernel`, 256 threads per block is fine for the large number of elements. For `attn_softmax_kernel`, I launch with `[B*NH, T]` blocks, 1 thread each. Since T=32, a single thread doing a loop of 32 is fine. But I could parallelize this more. With 32 threads per block doing a parallel reduction for softmax... Eh, 12288 blocks of 1 thread each is a lot of blocks. On H100, that's fine since each block is tiny and SM occupancy will be high. Actually, 12288 blocks is exactly 1 block per attention head on H100 with 132 SMs? H100 has 132 SMs. 12288 / 132 ≈ 93 blocks per SM. For a tiny kernel that does ~100 operations, this is fine. The blocks will execute in waves. But can I make it slightly more efficient? Maybe use 1 warp (32 threads) per block and parallelize the softmax computation: - Thread i in warp reads element j = i from the row - Use warp-level reduction for max - Compute exp(x - max) - Warp-level reduction for sum - Divide by sum - Write back For T=32, a single warp can handle it perfectly. Let me rewrite the softmax kernel: ```cpp __global__ void attn_softmax_kernel(float* scores, int batch, int T_val, float scale) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; if (b >= batch || i >= T_val) return; float* row = scores + b * T_val * T_val + i * T_val; // Warp size is 32, T_val is 32. Perfect fit. int tid = threadIdx.x; float val = (tid <= i) ? row[tid] * scale : -1e30f; // Warp max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset)); } float max_val = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 0); // Exp val = (tid <= i) ? expf(row[tid] * scale - max_val) : 0.0f; // Warp sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } float sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 0); if (tid <= i) { row[tid] = val / sum; } else { row[tid] = 0.0f; } } ``` Launch as `dim3(batch, T_val), 32`. This is more efficient and elegant. For the `transpose_0213_kernel`, a simple element-wise copy is fine. For all the other kernels, let me verify the grid/block dimensions: - `embed_kernel`: `dim3(B, T), 256` - good - `layer_norm_kernel`: `B * T, 256` - good - `add_bias_kernel`: `B * T, 256` - good - `add_kernel`: `(N + 255) / 256, 256` - good - `gelu_kernel`: `(N + 255) / 256, 256` - good - `add_bias_gelu_kernel`: `B * T, 256` - good - `split_qkv_kernel`: `(B*T*3*D + 255) / 256, 256` - good - `transpose_0213_kernel`: `(B*T*D + 255) / 256, 256` - good Now let me think about the `add_bias_and_residual` kernels. I'll use them after the c_proj and mlp_c_proj. ```cpp __global__ void add_bias_residual_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* bias, const float* residual, int N, int D_val) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; float* o = out + n * D_val; const float* x = in + n * D_val; const float* r = residual + n * D_val; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D_val; i += blockDim.x) { o[i] = x[i] + bias[i] + r[i]; } } ``` Launch: `N, 256` I also need a kernel for simple bias addition after the MLP up-projection, before GELU: Actually, my `add_bias_gelu_kernel` does both. For the attention c_proj: `add_bias_residual_kernel`. For the MLP c_proj: `add_bias_residual_kernel`. For the QKV projection: `add_bias_kernel`. One more optimization: the `add_bias_kernel` writes `out = in + bias`. If I do this in-place on the cuBLAS output, I can just pass `in` and `out` pointing to the same buffer. In cuBLAS, I compute `C = A @ B`, writing to `qkv.data_ptr()`. Then `add_bias_kernel` reads and writes to the same buffer. That's fine because `add_bias_kernel` does `o[i] = x[i] + bias[i]`. If `o` and `x` are the same pointer, it becomes `o[i] = o[i] + bias[i]`. Perfect. Similarly, after c_proj GEMM, the output is in `buf2`. Then `add_bias_residual(buf2, buf2, c_proj_b, buf0)` computes `buf2 = buf2 + c_proj_b + buf0`. And after mlp_c_proj GEMM, output is in `buf0`. Then `add_bias_residual(buf0, buf0, mlp_c_proj_b, buf2)` computes `buf0 = buf0 + mlp_c_proj_b + buf2`. This is exactly what we want. Now let me write the complete CUDA source. I'll be very careful. Actually, I just realized an issue with my buffer layout. After `cublas(qkv, buf1, c_attn_w)`, `qkv` is [B*T, 3*D]. Then `split_qkv` reads from `qkv` and writes to `q`, `k`, `v`. After that, `qkv` is no longer needed until the next layer. Similarly, `attn_scores` is [B*NH, T, T], `attn_out` is [B*NH, T, HS]. For the transpose after attention: `transpose_0213(buf1, attn_out)` writes to `buf1`. Then `cublas(buf2, buf1, c_proj_w)`. One concern: `buf1` is `B*T*D` floats. After `transpose_0213`, it contains [B, T, D]. But this buffer was used for `ln1(buf0)` earlier in the layer. After `split`, it's no longer needed for Q,K,V (since q,k,v have their own buffers). So overwriting `buf1` is safe. Actually, I need to double-check the `split_qkv` step. `qkv` is [32768, 2304]. `q`, `k`, `v` are each [12288, 32, 64]. `split_qkv` reads from `qkv` and writes to `q`, `k`, `v` in separate buffers. Then `cublas(attn_scores, q, k)` uses `q` and `k`. `cublas(attn_out, attn_scores, v)` uses `v`. `buf1` is NOT used during `split` through `cublas`. Great. Then after `attn_out`, `transpose_0213(buf1, attn_out)` writes to `buf1`. Then `cublas(buf2, buf1, c_proj_w)` reads `buf1` and writes to `buf2`. Then `add_bias_residual(buf2, buf2, c_proj_b, buf0)`. Then `layer_norm(buf1, buf2)`. Then MLP... All good. Now let me write out the complete code. Wait, one more thing. What if I want to ensure the tensors passed to C++ are contiguous? I should call `.contiguous()` in Python. Also, for the embedding, `input_ids` might be int64. The `embed_kernel` expects `int64_t`. In the kernel, I cast to `int` for indexing. For `wte` and `wpe`, their shapes are [vocab_size, D] and [n_positions, D]. For `wpe`, I might only need the first T positions. But I pass the whole tensor and index by `t`. reasoning
The build failed because I included CUDA headers in `cpp_sources` instead of `cuda_sources`. The `load_inline` function requires `cuda_sources` to contain actual CUDA code and `cpp_sources` for pure C++ host code that interfaces with PyTorch. Let me restructure the code properly. The load_inline call should have `cpp_sources` as a C++ wrapper that calls the CUDA kernel, or I can put everything in `cuda_sources`. Actually, wait. I should also think about whether this approach can actually beat PyTorch. For small T=32 and large B=1024: - The naive layer norm kernel performs global memory reductions, which is very inefficient compared to PyTorch's optimized layer norm kernel. - The attention with T=32 is tiny, and PyTorch likely has highly optimized implementations. - My QKV split and transpose add extra memory traffic. Given H100 has 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth and the model weights are about 124M parameters * 4 bytes = 496 MB. For each layer, we have weights: - c_attn: 768*2304 + 2304 - c_proj: 768*768 + 768 - c_fc: 768*3072 + 3072 - c_proj_mlp: 3072*768 + 768 - layer_norm: 2*768 + 2*768 = 3072 Total per layer: ~3.5M params = 14 MB 12 layers: ~168 MB Plus embeddings, final LN, lm_head: ~500 MB total For batch=1024, seq=32, the activation size per layer is small (1024*32*768*4 = 100MB). However, the layer norm is a concern. Let me optimize it slightly by using warp-level parallel reductions instead of the naive loop. For D=768, with 256 threads per block: - Each thread handles 3 elements (768/256 = 3). - We can compute partial sums per thread, then do a warp reduce, then block reduce. - This is much faster than each thread looping over 768 elements. Actually, in my original kernel: ```c float mean = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) mean += x[i]; ``` Each thread in the block (256 threads) loops over all 768 elements. That's horrible! Not only is it doing redundant work (256 threads each computing the same mean), but it's also uncoalesced memory access since all threads access the same memory locations simultaneously. Wait, actually looking more carefully, the kernel is launched with 1 block per token and 256 threads per block. But ALL threads in the block read the same x[i] in the loop! This is incredibly inefficient - it's doing 256x redundant work and creating massive memory contention. I need to rewrite the layer norm kernel properly. A better approach: ```c __global__ void layer_norm_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int N, int D) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const float* x = in + n * D; float* y = out + n * D; // Each thread handles a subset of elements float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { sum += x[i]; } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } // Block reduce using shared memory __shared__ float s_mean; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { s_mean = sum / D; } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean; // Variance float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { float d = x[i] - mean; var_sum += d * d; } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset); } __shared__ float s_var; if (threadIdx.x == 0) { s_var = var_sum / D; } __syncthreads(); float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var + 1e-5f); for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { y[i] = (x[i] - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } ``` This is much better. With 256 threads and D=768, each thread handles 3 elements initially, then we do warp/block reductions. But for the warp reduce to work correctly with blockDim.x=256, I need to reduce across all warps. Using `__shfl_down_sync` only reduces within a warp. I need to write to shared memory and reduce across warps. Actually, let me use a simpler approach with shared memory: ```c __device__ inline float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void layer_norm_kernel(float* out, const float* in, const float* gamma, const float* beta, int N, int D) { int n = blockIdx.x; if (n >= N) return; const float* x = in + n * D; float* y = out + n * D; __shared__ float s_mean, s_var; float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < D; i += blockDim.x) { sum += x[i]; } sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { // atomic add to shared memory or write to smem for warp 0 } // Actually, we need block-level reduction } ``` For block-level reduction across 8 warps (256 threads), I could use atomicAdd on shared memory: ```c __shared__ float smem[1]; if (threadIdx.x == 0) smem[0] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); float sum = ... // partial sum per thread sum = warp_reduce_sum(sum); if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) atomicAdd(&smem[0], sum); __syncthreads(); float mean = smem[0] / D; ``` This is clean and efficient. For variance, same pattern. Let me update all kernels to be more efficient. Actually, for the element-wise kernels (add_bias, add_bias_residual, add_bias_gelu), my original code uses each thread looping with stride `blockDim.x`, which is fine since it's coalesced and there's no warp divergence. When I passed `attn_scores.data_ptr<float>()` to cublas with `lda=T`, cublas interprets each matrix as T x T column-major. In column-major, element (i, j) is at position `j*T + i`. Let me think carefully. `attn_scores` is allocated with PyTorch as `{B_CONST * NH_CONST, T_CONST, T_CONST}`. PyTorch stores tensors in row-major (or more accurately, the last dimension is contiguous). So accessing `attn_scores[b, i, j]` in Python gives the element at position `b*T*T + i*T + j`. No, that's not right. Let me reconsider: in cublas, for `transa=T, transb=N`: op(A) = K^T (T x hs) op(B) = Q (hs x T) C = K^T @ Q In standard matrix multiplication: C[i][j] = sum_k K^T[i][k] * Q[k][j] = sum_k K[k][i] * Q[k][j]. This is the dot product of the i-th column of K and the j-th column of Q. But in attention, we want scores[i][j] = dot(Q_i, K_j) where Q_i is the i-th row (or token's query vector) and K_j is the j-th key vector. In my memory layout, Q and K are stored with each "column" being a token. In column-major, element (k, j) of Q corresponds to the k-th head dim of the j-th token. So the j-th column of Q is Q_j. So C[i][j] = dot(Q_j, K_i). In row-major terms, C[row=i][col=j] gives attention score for query j attending to key i. But that means the matrix is transposed from what we expect! Actually, the standard attention score is Q @ K^T, where Q is (T, hs) and K^T is (hs, T). The result is (T, T) where entry (i, j) = dot(Q_i, K_j). But cublas computes C = K^T @ Q, which is also (T, T). And C[i][j] = dot(K_i, Q_j) = dot(Q_j, K_i). So C[i][j] = standard_scores[j][i]. The matrix C is the transpose of what we want. When cublas writes C in column-major: - In memory, element at position `p` corresponds to column-major index (i, j) if `p = j*T + i`. - The logical value at (i, j) in column-major is `C[i][j] = dot(K_i, Q_j)`. - We want row-major `scores[i][j] = dot(Q_i, K_j)`. So we need: `scores[i][j] = C[j][i] = dot(K_j, Q_i)`. This means if we interpret the cublas output as a row-major matrix, the element at row i, column j is actually the value we want! Let me verify: If we store a T x T matrix in row-major: position `i*T + j` contains the value for row i, col j. If we want row-major position `i*T + j` to contain `scores(i, j) = dot(Q_i, K_j)`, and we know cublas writes `C(i, j) = dot(K_i, Q_j) = dot(Q_j, K_i) = scores(j, i)` to position `j*T + i`. This means A is m x k = 2304 x 768 in column-major. c_attn_w for Conv1D is (768, 2304) row-major, which in column-major is (2304, 768). Correct. B = ln_out, ldb = 768. B is k x n = 768 x 32768 in column-major. ln_out is (32768, 768) row-major, which in column-major is (768, 32768). Correct. C = qkv, ldc = 2304. C is m x n = 2304 x 32768 in column-major. Correct. For c_proj: ```c cublasSgemm(handle, CUBLAS_OP_N, CUBLAS_OP_N, D_CONST, B_CONST * T_CONST, D_CONST, &alpha, c_proj_w, D_CONST, ln_out, D_CONST, &beta, attn_proj, D_CONST); ``` m=768, n=32768, k=768. A=c_proj_w (768, 768) row-major = (768, 768) col-major. lda=768. Correct. B=ln_out (768, 32768) col-major. ldb=768. Correct. C=attn_proj (768, 32768) col-major. ldc=768. Correct. All good. Now, let me also consider if there might be issues with buffer reuse and data corruption. In each layer: - `x` is buf0 - `ln_out` is buf1 - `attn_proj` is buf2 For layer 0: x=buf0 contains embeddings. After MLP: x=buf0 contains the layer output. For layer 1: x=buf0 is used as input, ln_out=buf1 for LN1 output, attn_proj=buf2 for c_proj output. Wait, after layer 0's attention, `attn_proj = buf2` contains the attention output (with bias + residual). Then after MLP: ```c cublasSgemm(..., x, ...); // writes to buf0 add_bias_residual_kernel(..., x, ..., attn_proj, ...); // adds bias and buf2 residual to buf0 ``` So after layer 0, `x = buf0` contains the layer output. Good. For layer 1: ```c layer_norm_kernel(..., ln_out, x, ...) // reads buf0, writes buf1 cublasSgemm(..., qkv, ..., ln_out, ...) // reads buf1, writes qkv ... cublasSgemm(..., attn_proj, ..., ln_out, ...) // reads buf1 (after transpose), writes buf2 add_bias_residual_kernel(..., attn_proj, ..., c_proj_b, x, ...) // reads buf2 and buf0, writes buf2 ... cublasSgemm(..., x, ..., mlp_mid, ...) // writes buf0 add_bias_residual_kernel(..., x, ..., mlp_c_proj_b, attn_proj, ...) // reads buf2 and buf0, writes buf0 ``` Wait, the MLP writes to `x=buf0` using `mlp_mid` and reads `attn_proj=buf2` as residual. That seems correct. One potential issue: after the attention c_proj, `attn_proj` is overwritten. But then the MLP's final add_bias_residual uses `attn_proj` as residual. So `attn_proj` must contain the attention output until after MLP. Looking at the code: ```c // After attention add_bias_residual_kernel(..., attn_proj, ..., c_proj_b, x, ...); // attn_proj = buf2 // MLP reads attn_proj for residual at the end ``` Between c_attn and the MLP final residual, do we overwrite `attn_proj`? Let me trace: 1. Attention c_proj writes to `attn_proj=buf2` 2. `add_bias_residual_kernel` writes to `attn_proj=buf2` (in-place, since out=attn_proj and in=attn_proj) 3. MLP starts with `layer_norm_kernel(..., ln_out, attn_proj, ...)` - reads `buf2`, writes `buf1` 4. `cublasSgemm(..., mlp_mid, ..., ln_out, ...)` - writes `mlp_mid` (separate buffer) 5. `cublasSgemm(..., x, ..., mlp_mid, ...)` - writes to `buf0` 6. `add_bias_residual_kernel(..., x, ..., mlp_c_proj_b, attn_proj, ...)` - reads `buf2`, writes `buf0` So `attn_proj=buf2` is never overwritten between step 2 and step 6. Good. OK, I'm now fairly confident the logic is correct. Let me also check the initial and final buffers: After the final layer (layer 11): - `x=buf0` contains the final layer output - `ln_out=buf1` is used for final layer norm and then LM head matmul - `attn_proj=buf2` might contain garbage but isn't used Then: ```c layer_norm_kernel(..., ln_out, x, ln_f_g, ln_f_b, ...); // reads buf0, writes buf1 cublasSgemm(..., logits, ..., ln_out, ...); // reads buf1, writes logits ``` This is correct. One more issue: `ln_out` after the final layer norm is used for the LM head. But in the loop, `ln_out` was used for intermediate values. After the final iteration of the loop, `ln_out` does NOT necessarily contain anything useful. But then we do the final LN which writes to `ln_out`. So it's fine. Wait, what if the loop finishes and `x=buf0` contains the layer output, but `ln_out=buf1` contains the old LN2 output from the last layer? Actually, let me simplify. For a Linear layer with weight shape `(out_features, in_features)`: - In PyTorch: `y = x @ W.t()` where x is `(N, in)`, W is `(out, in)`, so W.t() is `(in, out)` - In cublas terms: `y^T = W @ x^T`, so we compute `C = A * B` where `A = W` (shape `out x in` in column-major) and `B = x^T` (shape `in x N` in column-major) - PyTorch W of shape `(out, in)` in row-major = cublas W of shape `(in, out)` in column-major - So we need `CUBLAS_OP_T` on A to get `out x in` in cublas terms - And `CUBLAS_OP_T` on B to get `in x N` to `N x in`... wait, no. Let's think about column-major layout. A matrix with shape (M, N) in row-major (C style) has element (i, j) at position `i*N + j`. In column-major (Fortran style), element (i, j) is at `j*M + i`. cublas uses column-major. So a PyTorch tensor `W` of shape `(out, in)` in row-major has `W[i, j]` at `i*in + j`. If we tell cublas it's a column-major matrix with `lda = out`, then: - Element (i, j) in cublas's interpretation is at `j*out + i` - So `W[pytorch_row=i, pytorch_col=j]` = `W_memory[i*in + j]` - In cublas, this same memory at position `j*out + i` corresponds to cublas_row=i, cublas_col=j - But `i*in + j = j*out + i` only in special cases This means a row-major `(out, in)` matrix is NOT a valid column-major `(out, in)` matrix unless `out = in = 1` (or it's square and symmetric in a specific way). The standard trick: if you want to multiply row-major matrices `A x B = C`, you can use cublas column-multiplication by transposing the roles: - `C^T = B^T * A^T` in column-major - So to compute `C = A * B` in row-major: - Set transA = CUBLAS_OP_T, transB = CUBLAS_OP_T - m = B_rows(A_cols)? Standard cublas Sgemm: ```c cublasSgemm(handle, transA, transB, m, n, k, alpha, A, lda, B, ldb, beta, C, ldc) ``` This computes `C = alpha * op(A) * op(B) + beta * C` where: - op(A) has dimensions `m x k` - op(B) has dimensions `k x n` - C has dimensions `m x n` - All matrices are stored in column-major. So how does PyTorch handle this? The trick is: a row-major matrix of shape `(M, K)` is a column-major matrix of shape `(K, M)` with transpose! - Element (i, j) in row-major MxK is at position `i*K + j` - Element (j, i) in column-major KxM is at position `i*K + j` - So row-major `(M, K)` = column-major `(K, M)^T` Therefore: - `weight` row-major `(M, K)` = cublas `op(A)` where A is column-major `(K, M)` and `op = transpose` - So if we pass `weight` as A with `transA = T`, then `op(A)` has shape `M x K` - `input` row-major `(N, K)` = cublas `op(B)` where B is column-major `(K, N)` and `op = no transpose` - Wait, `input` row-major `(N, K)` = column-major `(K, N)^T`. Let me reconsider. A row-major `(N, K)` matrix has elements at memory position `i*K + j` for row `i`, col `j`. In cublas column-major, a matrix with `m` rows and `n` columns has element at `(i, j)` stored at `j*m + i` for `0 <= i < m`, `0 <= j < n`. If we have a PyTorch row-major `(N, K)` matrix, and we want cublas to interpret it as a column-major `K x N` matrix with NO transpose: - cublas sees `m=K` rows, `n=N` columns - Element `(i, j)` in cublas (where `0 <= i < K`, `0 <= j < N`) is at memory position `j*K + i` - PyTorch row `j`, col `i` is at memory position `j*K + i` - This exactly matches! row-major `(N, K)` = column-major `(K, N)` with NO transpose. Wait, that's brilliant! A row-major matrix of shape `(N, K)` is exactly a column-major matrix of shape `(K, N)`. No transpose needed! Similarly: - row-major `(M, K)` = column-major `(K, M)` with NO transpose. - row-major `(N, M)` = column-major `(M, N)` with NO transpose. I think the fundamental issue is that when we translate col-major to row-major, the matrix MULTIPLICATION operation changes. In col-major, `A * B = C` means each element `C[i,j] = sum_k A[i,k] * B[k,j]`. In row-major, if we just reinterpret the memory, the formula for element access changes.
